PMC2784897.nxml.gz	42	['Although each method has its own limitations (described in detail elsewhere, e.g., Plomin, DeFries, McClearn, & McGuffin, in press), these limitations are generally complementary.']	(8335, 8514)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	43	['Consequently, the convergence of results from the two methods provides strong evidence for the validity of the findings.']	(8515, 8635)

PMC2784897.nxml.gz	45	['The method is based on comparison between identical and nonidentical twins.']	(8826, 8901)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	46	['Identical or monozygotic (MZ) twins derive from one zygote and are genetically identical.']	(8902, 8991)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	47	['If genetic factors are important for a trait, these genetically identical pairs of individuals must be more similar than first-degree relatives, who are only 50% similar genetically on average.']	(8992, 9185)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	48	['The best comparison group among first-degree relatives for MZ twins is dizygotic (DZ) twins, who develop from separately fertilized eggs.']	(9186, 9323)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	49	['Half of DZ twins are same-sex pairs and half are opposite-sex pairs.']	(9324, 9392)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	50	['Like MZ twins, DZ twins experience together most prenatal and many postnatal experiential variables such as prenatal nutrition and family social class.']	(9393, 9544)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	51	['If a trait is influenced genetically, identical twins must be more similar than fraternal twins.']	(9545, 9641)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	52	['However, when greater similarity of MZ twins is found, it is also possible that some or all of the greater similarity is caused environmentally rather than genetically.']	(9642, 9810)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	53	['The equal environment assumption of the twin method assumes that environmentally caused similarity is roughly the same for both types of twins.']	(9811, 9954)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	54	['If the assumption were violated because identical twins experience more similar environments and consequently develop more similarly than nonidentical twins, this violation would inflate estimates of genetic influence.']	(9955, 10173)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	55	['There is, in fact, evidence that MZ twins are treated more similarly than their DZ counterparts.']	(10174, 10270)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	56	['For example, as children, MZ twins are more likely to have the same playmates, share the same room, and dress alike.']	(10271, 10387)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	57	['As adults, MZ twins are more likely to keep in contact than are same-sex DZ twins (Evans & Martins, 2000).']	(10388, 10494)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	58	['However, the equal environment assumption would only be violated if this greater similarity for MZ twins leads to a greater similarity for phenotypes of interest.']	(10495, 10657)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	59	['The equal environments assumption has been tested in several ways and appears reasonable for most traits.']	(10658, 10763)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	60	['For example, environmental similarity during childhood does not predict twin similarity in personality, attitudes, intelligence, nor a range of psychiatric disorders (Evans & Martins, 2000).']	(10764, 10954)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	61	['Moreover, both greater similarity of parental treatment of MZ twins and greater physical similarity between MZ twins are uncorrelated with twin similarity for personality, vocational interests, and cognitive abilities.']	(10955, 11173)

PMC2784897.nxml.gz	65	['However, it is important to remember that statistics derived from twin data, which estimate genetic influences (heritability) and environmental influences, have very specific definitions within the twin method (see Chapter II), and can be misinterpreted.']	(11744, 11998)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	66	['For example, heritability refers to effect size, the extent to which individual differences for the trait in the population can be accounted for by genetic differences among individuals.']	(11999, 12185)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	67	['Effect size in this sense refers to individual differences for a trait in the entire population, not to the effect of genetic factors on a specific individual (Plomin et al., in press).']	(12186, 12371)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	68	['In other words, heritability is the proportion of phenotypic variance that can be accounted for by genetic differences among individuals.']	(12372, 12509)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	69	['Like all statistics, heritability estimates include error of estimation, which is a function of the effect size and the sample size.']	(12510, 12642)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	70	['Therefore, as with other methods, replication across studies and across designs is crucial.']	(12643, 12734)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	71	['It should be emphasized that heritability refers to the contribution of genetic differences to observed differences among individuals within a specific population, for a particular trait, and at a particular time.']	(12735, 12948)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	72	['Moreover, heritability describes what is in a particular population at a particular time rather than what could be .']	(12949, 13065)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	73	['That is, if either genetic influences change (e.g., changes due to migration) or environmental influences change (e.g., changes in curricula or in educational opportunity), then the relative impact of genes and environment will also change.']	(13066, 13306)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	74	['Even for a highly heritable trait such as height, changes in the environment could make a big difference.']	(13307, 13412)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	75	["For example, if an epidemic struck or if children's diets were altered for the worse by famine, average height would decrease, but genetic influence might actually increase due to diminished environmental variance."]	(13413, 13627)

PMC2784897.nxml.gz	138	['In these analyses, group heritability refers to genetic influence on the average difference between the low-performing group and the rest of the population.']	(24686, 24842)

PMC2784897.nxml.gz	141	['One of the goals of the present monograph is to investigate genetic and environmental influences on learning abilities in a large and representative sample, which is the focus of Chapter III.']	(25275, 25466)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	142	['For the first time, we systematically compare estimates of genetic and environmental influences on individual differences across the full distribution of ability with estimates of those influences for low-performing children within the same sample assessed on the same measures at the same ages.']	(25467, 25762)

PMC2784897.nxml.gz	145	['A feature of DF extremes analysis is that, by combining qualitative information about proband status and quantitative variation in cotwins, it can clarify genetic and environmental links between the abnormal and the normal.']	(26158, 26381)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	146	['This second question is the focus of Chapter IV.']	(26382, 26430)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	147	['DEVELOPMENTAL CONTINUITY AND CHANGE\nTo what extent do genetic and environmental influences on learning abilities and disabilities change during development?']	(26432, 26588)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	148	['There are two questions here—a question about quantitative differences in the magnitude of genetic and environmental influences and a question about qualitative changes in genetic and environmental influences.']	(26589, 26798)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	149	['The first question about quantitative differences can be addressed with cross-sectional data.']	(26799, 26892)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	150	['The second question about qualitative changes from age to age requires longitudinal data.']	(26893, 26982)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	151	['Concerning quantitative age differences, genetic research on “ g ” has yielded two fascinating developmental trends.']	(26983, 27099)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	152	['First, heritability increases linearly from about 20% in infancy, to about 40% in middle childhood, to about 50% in adolescence and young adulthood, and even higher in middle age (Boomsma, 1993;McGue, Bouchard, Jr., Iacono, & Lykken, 1993;Plomin, 1986).']	(27100, 27353)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	153	['The cause of this developmental increase in heritability is not known but one possibility is that as children increasingly make their own way in the world they move from experiencing environments largely created by other people to actively creating correlations between their genetic propensities and their experiences (Plomin & DeFries, 1985).']	(27354, 27698)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	154	['Second, shared environmental influence decreases sharply from about 30% in childhood to near 0% in adolescence, perhaps as adolescents increasingly live their lives outside their family.']	(27699, 27885)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	155	['To the extent that academic achievement reflects “ g ,” similar developmental trends would be expected for learning abilities and disabilities.']	(27886, 28029)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	156	['Although there are few studies of learning abilities, and their measures and samples differ considerably, the results reviewed in the previous section on learning abilities suggest a trend in this same direction.']	(28030, 28242)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	157	['The only study in the early school years (middle childhood) yielded estimates of 40% heritability and 40% shared environment (Thompson et al., 1991).']	(28243, 28392)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	158	['In early adolescence, two studies yielded average estimates of about 55% heritability and 30% shared environment (Bartels et al., 2002b;Husén, 1959).']	(28393, 28542)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	159	['In late adolescence, two studies yielded average estimates of about 50% heritability and 20% shared environment (Loehlin & Nichols, 1976;Wainwright, Wright, Geffen, Luciano, & Martin, 2005a;Wainwright et al., 2005b).']	(28543, 28759)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	160	['Nonetheless, although such cross-sectional comparisons across studies with different samples and measures can provide rough estimates of developmental differences in genetic and environmental influences, what is needed for precise comparisons is a longitudinal study with the same samples and measures at each age.']	(28760, 29074)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	161	['It should also be noted that comparing genetic and environmental estimates across ages requires large samples.']	(29075, 29185)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	162	['For example, in a sample of 200 pairs of twins, a heritability estimate of 40% is surrounded by a 95% confidence interval of 5–70%, which means that it has no power to compare heritability estimates with another study.']	(29186, 29404)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	163	['Twin studies are especially underpowered to detect and compare estimates of shared environmental influence (Hopper, 2000).']	(29405, 29527)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	164	['The second question about qualitative changes in genetic and environmental influence from age to age requires longitudinal data.']	(29528, 29656)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	165	['Rather than asking how much do genetic and environmental factors affect performance, here we are asking a logically independent question: To what extent are the same genetic and environmental factors influential at different ages?']	(29657, 29887)

PMC2784897.nxml.gz	337	['A set of adaptive branching rules was developed separately for each of three subtests, so that all the children started with the same items, but then were branched to easier or harder items depending on their performance.']	(61146, 61367)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	338	['The presentation of items was streamed, so that items from the three subtests were mixed to make the test more interesting, but the data recording and branching were done within each subtest.']	(61368, 61559)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	339	['Participants could attempt each item only once.']	(61560, 61607)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	340	["As with many psychological tests that use branching (e.g., Wechsler Intelligence Scale for Children (WISC-III-UK, Wechsler, 1992)), the general scoring rules were as follows: 1 point was recorded for each correct response, for each unadministered item preceding the child's starting point, and for each item skipped through branching to harder items."]	(61608, 61958)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	341	['After a certain number of failures, a discontinuation rule was applied within each area, and no points were recorded for all items after discontinuation.']	(61959, 62112)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	342	['Thus, for each of the 77 items, a score of 1 or 0 was recorded for each child.']	(62113, 62191)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	343	['For example, for Computation and Knowledge (total number of items=31), all children started at item 10.']	(62192, 62295)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	344	['The following rules were then applied:\nIf items 10–12 were all answered incorrectly, the child was branched to item 1, and had to continue with the test attempting all remaining items, or until the discontinuation criterion was met.']	(62296, 62528)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	345	['If items 10–12 were all answered correctly, the child received credit for all preceding items (1–9), and was branched to item 24.']	(62529, 62658)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	346	['If items 24–26 were all answered incorrectly, the child was branched back to item 13 and had to continue with the test (skipping all items administered previously), attempting all remaining items, or until the discontinuation criterion was met.']	(62659, 62903)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	347	['If one or two of items 24–26 were answered incorrectly the child received credit for all preceding items (13–23) and then continued with the test, attempting items 27–31, or until the discontinuation criterion was met.']	(62904, 63122)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	348	['If items 10–12 were not all answered incorrectly or correctly (i.e., if some but not all were answered correctly), the child received credit for all preceding items (1–9) and then had to continue with the test, attempting at all remaining items or until the discontinuation criterion was met.']	(63123, 63415)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	349	['Discontinuation criterion: three incorrect answers in a row (does not apply across branching points).']	(63416, 63517)

PMC2784897.nxml.gz	416	['GENETIC ANALYSES\nThe twin method, one of the major tools of quantitative genetic research, addresses the origins of individual differences by estimating the proportion of variance that can be attributed to genetic, shared environment, and nonshared environment factors (Plomin et al., in press).']	(82449, 82744)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	417	['In the case of complex traits that are likely to be influenced by multiple factors, the genetic component of variance refers to the influence of alleles at all gene loci that affect the trait.']	(82745, 82937)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	418	['The similarity between twins for any particular trait can be due wholly or in part to these shared genetic effects.']	(82938, 83053)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	419	['Twin similarity may also be due wholly or in part to shared environment, which refers to environmental influences that vary in the population but are experienced similarly by members of pairs of twins.']	(83054, 83255)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	420	['For example, pairs of twins experience similar conditions during gestation, have the same socio-economic status, live in the same family, and usually go to the same school.']	(83256, 83428)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	421	['These factors could reasonably be expected to increase similarity between co-twins.']	(83429, 83512)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	422	['Nonshared environment refers to any aspect of environmental influence that is experienced differently by the two twins and contributes to phenotypic differences between them, including measurement error.']	(83513, 83716)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	423	['Such influences involve aspects of experience that are specific to an individual, such as traumas and diseases, idiosyncratic experiences, different peers, differential treatment by the parents and teachers, and, importantly, different perceptions of such experiences, even if the events appear to be ostensibly the same for the two children.']	(83717, 84059)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	424	['Genetic influence can be estimated by comparing intraclass correlations for identical (monozygotic, MZ) twins, who are genetically identical, and fraternal (dizygotic, DZ) twins, whose genetic relatedness is on average .50.']	(84060, 84283)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	425	['The phenotypic variance of a trait can be attributed to genetic variance to the extent that the MZ twin correlation exceeds the DZ twin correlation.']	(84284, 84432)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	426	['Specifically, heritability, which is the proportion of phenotypic variance attributed to genetic variance, can be estimated as twice the difference between the MZ and DZ twin correlations.']	(84433, 84621)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	427	['The relatedness for shared (common) environmental influences is assumed to be 1.0 for both MZ and DZ twin pairs who grow up in the same family because they experience equally similar prenatal and postnatal environments.']	(84622, 84841)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	428	["Shared environmental influences are evidenced to the extent that the DZ twins' correlation is more than half of the MZ correlation."]	(84842, 84973)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	429	['Limitations of the twin method can be found elsewhere (e.g.,Plomin, DeFries, McClearn, & McGuffin, 2001).']	(84974, 85079)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	430	['Twin correlations for all of the measures at all of the ages are presented in Chapter III.']	(85080, 85170)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	431	['Model Fitting\nStructural equation model fitting is a comprehensive way of estimating variance components of a given trait (or, as explained below, of the covariance between traits) based on the principles described above.']	(85172, 85393)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	432	['The fundamental quantitative genetic model is the so-called ACE model.']	(85394, 85464)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	433	['It apportions the phenotypic variance into genetic (A), shared environmental (C), and nonshared environmental (E) components, assuming no effects of nonadditive genetics or nonrandom mating.Figure 1illustrates the basic logic of this method.']	(85465, 85706)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	434	['The path coefficients of latent variables A (genetic), C (shared environmental), and E (nonshared environmental, including error of measurement) are represented by the lowercase letters a, c, and e, respectively.']	(85707, 85919)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	435	['Genetic relatedness is 1.0 for MZ twins and .5 for DZ twins.']	(85920, 85980)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	436	['Shared environmental relatedness is assumed to be 1.0 for both MZ and DZ twins.']	(85981, 86060)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	437	['The ACE parameters and their confidence intervals can be estimated by fitting the models to variance/covariance matrices using the model-fitting program Mx (Neale, 1997).']	(86061, 86231)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	438	['Caption (FIG): FIGURE 1\n\n—The basic twin model.']	(86233, 86280)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	439	['A, additive genetic influence; C, shared environment; E, nonshared environment; paths a, c, and e, effects of A, C, and E on a trait; rMZ, monozygotic genetic or shared environmental correlation; rDZ, dizygotic generic or shared environmental correlation.']	(86281, 86536)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	440	['ACE model-fitting results of individual differences for the entire sample for all measures at all ages are presented in Chapter III.']	(86537, 86669)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	441	['Sex-Limitation Models\nAs summarized inTable 9, there are three possibilities with respect to the causes of individual differences in boys and girls, regardless of mean differences between the sexes (Neale & Maes, 2003).']	(86671, 86890)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	442	['The first possibility is that different genetic and environmental factors are responsible for individual differences in mathematics for boys and girls—these are called qualitative differences.']	(86891, 87083)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	443	['Such sex-specific effects are not limited to genes on the X chromosome but can also involve genes on the autosomal chromosomes that affect boys and girls differently, for example, because the genes interact with sex hormones.']	(87084, 87309)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	444	['The second possibility, not mutually exclusive with the first, is that the same etiological influences affect individual differences in boys and girls, but that they do so to a different extent—these are known as quantitative differences.']	(87310, 87548)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	445	['The third possibility is that there are no differences in the etiology of individual differences for boys and girls; the same genes and environments operate to the same extent in both sexes, even if there are mean differences between boys and girls.']	(87549, 87798)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	446	['That is, mean reading scores are lower for boys than girls, but the factors that make one boy different from another can be the same as those that make one girl different from another girl.']	(87799, 87988)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	447	['It should be noted that quantitative genetics with its focus on individual differences has little to say about the origins of mean differences between boys and girls.']	(87989, 88155)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	448	['Indeed, we frequently find no quantitative or qualitative differences in the etiology of individual differences for boys and girls despite large mean differences (Viding et al., 2004).']	(88156, 88340)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	449	['Caption (TABLE-WRAP): TABLE 9\n\nTHREE POSSIBILITIES WITH RESPECT TO THE CAUSES OF INDIVIDUAL DIFFERENCES IN BOYS AND GIRLS, REGARDLESS OF MEAN DIFFERENCES BETWEEN THE SEXESSex Differences in Etiology of Individual DifferencesExplanationPossible Contributing Factors Qualitative differencesDifferent genetic and environmental factors are responsible for individual differences for boys and girls.Genes on the sex chromosomes.']	(88342, 88765)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	450	['Genes on the autosomal chromosomes affect boys and girls differently, for example, because the genes interact with sex hormones.']	(88766, 88894)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	451	['Teachers treat boys and girls differently in terms of their expectations or requests for help.']	(88895, 88989)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	452	['Quantitative differencesThe same etiological influences affect individual differences in boys and girls, but that they do so to a different extent.As above, but the differences are in quantity of effects.No differences in etiologyThe same genes and environments operate to the same extent in both sexes.Boys as a group may exhibit a mean disadvantage, but the factors that make one boy different from another are the same as those that make one girl different from another girl.']	(88990, 89468)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	453	['These three possibilities (qualitative differences, quantitative differences, and no differences) can be assessed using sex-limitation structural equation modeling.']	(89469, 89633)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	454	['Each possibility is associated with a set of parameters in the sex-limitation models (seeFigure 2).']	(89634, 89733)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	455	['Qualitative differences are evidenced in the genetic relatedness ( rg) between DZ opposite-sex twins.']	(89734, 89835)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	456	['In DZ same-sex pairs, the assumption is that on average the twins share 50% of their varying DNA, and the coefficient of genetic relatedness is therefore .5.']	(89836, 89993)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	457	['If there are qualitative differences in etiology between boys and girls (different genetic and environmental factors), the genetic relatedness in DZ opposite-sex twins will be less than .5.']	(89994, 90183)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	458	['If there are quantitative differences (the same factors, but exerting different magnitudes of effect) rather than qualitative differences, the genetic relatedness for DZ opposite-sex pairs will still be .5, but the parameter estimates for the A, C, and E components will be significantly different for male–male pairs and female–female pairs.']	(90184, 90526)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	459	['If there are no qualitative or quantitative differences between boys and girls, the genetic relatedness of DZ opposite-sex (DZos) pairs will be .5 and the A, C, and E estimates for male–male and female–female pairs will be the same.']	(90527, 90759)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	460	['However, the phenotypic variance might nonetheless differ for the two sexes because mean differences are often associated with variance differences (i.e., higher means have higher variances).']	(90760, 90951)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	461	['Caption (FIG): FIGURE 2\n\n—Full sex-limitation model (a) and nested models (b and c) Note.— A, additive genetic influence; C, shared environment influence; E, nonshared environment influence; Paths a, c, and e, effects of A, C, and E on a trait with subscript “m” for males and “f” for females; rg, genetic relatedness between same-sex twins which is fixed at 1.0 for MZ twins and .5 for DZ twins; rc, shared environment relatedness between same-sex twins which is fixed at 1.0 for MZ and DZ twins; rgO, genetic relatedness between opposite-sex twins; rcO, shared environment relatedness between opposite-sex twins.']	(90953, 91567)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	462	['Opposite-sex twins are represented as twin one male and twin two female, and are linked by rgO and rcO.']	(91568, 91671)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	463	['(a) shows the full sex limitation model that estimates seven parameters: am, cm, em, af, cf, ef, and rgO or rcO.']	(91672, 91784)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	464	['This model allows qualitative sex differences in that the genetic and shared environmental correlations ( rgO and rcO) between opposite-sex twins are allowed to be <.5 and 1.0, respectively.']	(91785, 91975)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	465	['The model also allows quantitative sex differences in that the ACE parameters for males and females ( am, cm, em, af, cf, ef) can differ.']	(91976, 92113)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	466	['Variances differences between the sexes are also allowed (not shown in the path diagram).']	(92114, 92203)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	467	['(b) shows the common effects model, which is nested in the full sex limitation model and tests for qualitative sex differences by constraining the genetic and shared environmental correlations between opposite-sex twins ( rGO and rCO) to be .5 and 1.0, respectively (i.e., the same as same-sex DZ twins).']	(92204, 92508)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	468	['This model estimates six parameters: am, cm, em, af, cf, ef, and allows variance differences between the sexes.']	(92509, 92620)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	469	['(c) shows the scalar and null models of the sex-limitation design which reduces to the basic twin model and thus, in comparison with the other models, tests for both qualitative and quantitative sex differences.']	(92621, 92832)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	470	['This model estimates three parameters: a , c , and e for the sexes combined.']	(92833, 92909)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	471	['In the scalar model, variance differences between the sexes are allowed.']	(92910, 92982)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	472	['In the null model the variances are equated across the sexes (not shown in the path diagram).']	(92983, 93076)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	473	['Using the model-fitting program Mx (Neale, 1997) for each composite measure, we first tested the full model which allows all parameters to vary: rgin the DZ opposite-sex pairs, A, C, and E estimates, and variance estimates (seeFigure 2a).']	(93077, 93315)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	474	['This was fit to variance/covariance matrices derived from the data.']	(93316, 93383)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	475	['A series of nested models was then tested.']	(93384, 93426)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	476	['The first nested model (Figure 2b) is called the common effects sex-limitation model that tests for qualitative sex differences by fixing rgto .5 in the DZos, but allows different A, C, E, and variance estimates.']	(93427, 93639)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	477	['The second nested model (Figure 2c) is called a scalar effects sex-limitation model that tests for quantitative sex differences by constraining A, C, and E parameters to be the same in boys and girls as well as constraining rgto .5 in the DZos; however, it allows differences in phenotypic variance between males and females.']	(93640, 93965)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	478	['The third and final nested model, called the null model (alsoFigure 2c), tests for variance differences between boys and girls by constraining all the parameters to be equal for males and females.']	(93966, 94162)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	479	['For each model, the ACE parameters and their confidence intervals were estimated.']	(94163, 94244)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	480	['The overall fit of each model was evaluated using the root mean square error of approximation (RMSEA), with lower values representing better fitting models.']	(94245, 94401)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	481	['Results of sex-limitation model fitting are presented in Chapter III.']	(94402, 94471)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	482	['Teacher Heterogeneity Model\nIn order to test whether being in the same classroom and having the same teacher affected the results of our analyses, we analyzed each of the composite scores separately for the two groups (same vs. different teacher for the two twins in the family).']	(94473, 94752)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	483	['After examining the pattern of twin correlations for the two groups, we performed model-fitting analyses to test whether the differences in estimates for the two groups were statistically significant.']	(94753, 94953)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	484	['The model used for this analysis was similar to that of the sex-limitation models used to test for quantitative sex differences.']	(94954, 95082)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	485	['The full model allowed A, C, and E parameters to vary between the groups.']	(95083, 95156)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	486	['The null model equated the A, C, and E parameters for the two groups.']	(95157, 95226)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	487	['The results from the teacher heterogeneity model are also described in Chapter III.']	(95227, 95310)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	488	['Note that in the case of teacher ratings, being in the same classroom includes the effects of a shared teaching experience and a shared rater, whereas for the test scores, being in the same classroom reflects a shared teaching experience only.']	(95311, 95554)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	489	['Extremes Analyses\nThe previous model-fitting sections focused on the analysis of individual differences for the entire sample; that is, ability rather than disability.']	(95556, 95723)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	490	['An important feature of TEDS is that its large community sample makes it possible to study disability in the context of ability by selecting children at the low end of the normal distribution.']	(95724, 95916)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	491	['In Chapter IV, we present results for all measures at 7, 9, and 10 years for children in the lowest 15% of the distribution.']	(95917, 96041)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	492	['For each of the measures, we defined probands as 5% and 15% of the whole sample, identifying statistically low performance on that measure.']	(96042, 96181)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	493	['As results for both cut-offs were generally similar, we only present the results from the 15% cut-off analyses, which provided greater power.']	(96182, 96323)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	494	['Probandwise concordances (the ratio of the number of probands in concordant pairs to the total number of probands) were calculated separately for each measure and each of the five sex-by-zygosity groups.']	(96324, 96527)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	495	['Probandwise concordances represent the risk that a co-twin of a proband is affected.']	(96528, 96612)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	496	['Greater MZ than DZ concordances suggest genetic influence, but unlike twin correlations, twin concordances cannot be used to estimate genetic and environmental parameters because they do not in themselves include information about the population incidence.']	(96613, 96869)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	497	['DF extremes analysis assesses genetic links between disability and ability by bringing together dichotomous diagnoses of disability and quantitative traits of ability.']	(96870, 97037)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	498	['Rather than assessing twin similarity in terms of individual differences on a quantitative trait of ability or in terms of concordance for a diagnostic cut-off, DF extremes analysis assesses twin similarity as the extent to which the mean standardized quantitative trait score of co-twins of selected extreme or diagnosed probands is below the population mean and approaches the mean standardized score of those probands (seePlomin & Kovas, 2005for detailed explanation of DF extremes analysis and for discussion of alternative methods).']	(97038, 97575)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	499	['This measure of twin similarity is called a group twin correlation (or transformed co-twin mean ) in DF extremes analysis because it focuses on the mean quantitative trait score of co-twins rather than individual differences.']	(97576, 97801)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	500	['Genetic influence is implied if group twin correlations are greater for MZ than for DZ twins, that is, if the mean standardized score of the co-twins is lower for MZ pairs than for DZ pairs.']	(97802, 97992)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	501	['Doubling the difference between MZ and DZ group twin correlations estimates the genetic contribution to the average phenotypic difference between the probands and the population.']	(97993, 98171)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	502	['The ratio between this genetic estimate and the phenotypic difference between the probands and the population is called group heritability .']	(98172, 98312)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	503	['It should be noted that group heritability does not refer to individual differences among the probands–the question is not why one proband is slightly more disabled than another but rather why the probands as a group have lower scores than the rest of the population.Figure 3illustrates the basic logic of the DF analysis.']	(98313, 98635)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	504	['Caption (FIG): FIGURE 3\n\n—DF extremes analysis assesses twin similarity as the extent to which the mean standardized quantitative trait score of co-twins is as low as the mean standardized score of selected extreme or diagnosed probands.']	(98637, 98874)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	505	['This measure of twin similarity is called a group twin correlation (or transformed co-twin mean ) in DF extremes analysis because it focuses on the mean quantitative trait score of co-twins rather than individual differences.']	(98875, 99100)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	506	['Although DF extremes group heritability can be estimated by doubling the difference in MZ and DZ group twin correlations (Plomin, 1991), DF extremes analysis is more properly conducted using a regression model (DeFries & Fulker, 1988).']	(99101, 99336)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	507	['The DF extremes model fits standardized scores for MZ and DZ twins to the regression equation, C = B1P + B2R + A , where C is the predicted score for the co-twin, P is the proband score, R is the coefficient of genetic relatedness (1.0 for MZ twins and .5 for DZ twins), and A is the regression constant.']	(99337, 99641)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	508	['B1is the partial regression of the co-twin score on the proband, an index of average MZ and DZ twin resemblance independent of B2.']	(99642, 99772)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	509	['The focus of DF extremes analysis is on B2.']	(99773, 99816)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	510	['B2is the partial regression of the co-twin score on R independent of B1.']	(99817, 99889)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	511	['It is equivalent to twice the difference between the means for MZ and DZ co-twins adjusted for differences between MZ and DZ probands.']	(99890, 100024)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	512	['In other words, B2is the genetic contribution to the phenotypic mean difference between the probands and the population.']	(100025, 100145)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	513	['Group heritability is estimated by dividing B2by the difference between the means for probands and the population.']	(100146, 100260)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	514	['Finding group heritability implies that, first, disability and ability are both heritable, and second, that there are genetic links between the disability and normal variation in the ability.']	(100261, 100452)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	515	['That is, group heritability itself, not the comparison between group heritability and the other estimates of heritability, indicates genetic links between disability and ability.']	(100453, 100631)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	516	['If a measure of extremes (or a diagnosis) were not linked genetically to a quantitative trait, group heritability would be zero.']	(100632, 100760)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	517	['For example, this situation could occur if a severe form of learning disability is due to a single-gene disorder that contributes little to normal variation in learning ability.']	(100761, 100938)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	518	['However, most researchers now believe that common disorders such as learning disabilities are caused by common genetic variants—the common disease/common variant hypothesis (Collins, Euyer, & Chakravarti, 1997)—rather than by a concatenation of rare single-gene disorders.']	(100939, 101211)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	519	['To the extent that the same genes contribute to learning disability and normal variation in learning ability, group heritability will be observed, although the magnitude of group heritability depends on the individual heritability for normal variation and the heritability of disability gleaned from concordances for disability.']	(101212, 101540)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	520	['The results of these DF extremes analyses are the topic of Chapter IV.']	(101541, 101611)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	521	['Longitudinal Analyses\nCross-sectional designs can be used to compare genetic and environmental estimates across age but are weakened by the use of different samples at each age.']	(101613, 101790)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	522	['One strength of a longitudinal design is that the same sample is studied at each age.']	(101791, 101876)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	523	['However, the most important benefit of a longitudinal design is that analyses of age-to-age change and continuity are possible, as in the previous example of longitudinal DF extremes analysis.']	(101877, 102069)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	524	['Prospective and retrospective longitudinal analyses can be performed using the multivariate twin methodology described in the following section.']	(102070, 102214)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	525	['Longitudinal analyses are described in Chapter V. In Chapter V, we also present, for the first time, an extension of DF extremes analysis to a trait assessed at two measurement occasions, following the approach described in the following section.']	(102215, 102461)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	526	['For longitudinal DF extremes analysis, we selected probands on the basis of reading scores at 7 years and analyzed their co-twins quantitative reading scores, not at 7 years, but at 10 years.']	(102462, 102653)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	527	['Multivariate Analyses\nThe principles of the twin method can be extended to determine the etiology of the covariance between different traits, which is called multivariate genetic analysis.']	(102655, 102843)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	528	['As mentioned in the previous section, longitudinal analysis is a special case of multivariate analysis in that it focuses on the etiology of the covariance between the same trait at different ages.']	(102844, 103041)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	529	['In contrast to univariate quantitative genetic analysis that decomposes the variance of a single trait into genetic and environmental sources of variance, multivariate genetic analysis decomposes the covariance between traits into genetic and environmental sources of covariance (Martin & Eaves, 1977).']	(103042, 103344)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	530	['In other words, multivariate genetic analysis assesses genetic and environmental factors responsible for the phenotypic correlation between two traits.']	(103345, 103496)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	531	['For example, if the same genes affect different traits (called pleiotropy), a genetic correlation will be observed between the traits.']	(103497, 103631)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	532	['For twin studies, multivariate genetic analysis is based on cross-trait twin correlations for two or more traits.']	(103632, 103745)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	533	["That is, rather than comparing one twin's score on variable X with the co-twin's score on the same variable X , one twin's X is correlated with the co-twin's Y ."]	(103746, 103907)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	534	['The phenotypic covariance between two traits is attributed wholly or in part to their genetic overlap to the extent that the MZ cross-trait twin correlation exceeds the DZ cross-trait twin correlation.']	(103908, 104109)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	535	["Shared environmental influences are indicated to the extent that DZ twins' correlation is more than half of the MZ correlation."]	(104110, 104237)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	536	['As with the univariate analyses, structural equation modeling, based on the same principles, is used as a more comprehensive way of estimating the proportion of covariance.Figure 4illustrates a typical model (called Cholesky decomposition) that tests for common and independent genetic and environmental effects on variance in two different traits.']	(104238, 104586)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	537	['The Cholesky procedure is similar to hierarchical regression analyses in nongenetic studies, where the independent contribution of a predictor variable is assessed after accounting for its shared variance with other predictor variables.']	(104587, 104823)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	538	['In the bivariate case, the first factor assesses genetic and shared and nonshared environmental influences on trait 1, some of which also influence trait 2.']	(104824, 104980)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	539	['The second factor estimates genetic and shared and nonshared environmental influences unique to trait 2.']	(104981, 105085)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	540	['The same logic applies to more than two factors.']	(105086, 105134)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	541	['Caption (FIG): FIGURE 4\n\n—A typical decomposition (Cholesky decomposition) model that tests for common and independent genetic and environmental effects on variance in two different traits.']	(105136, 105325)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	542	['The same logic applies to the longitudinal analyses, where variable 1 and 2 are replaced with the same variable assessed at ages 1 and 2.']	(105326, 105463)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	543	['Another important statistic that can be derived from Cholesky analyses is bivariate heritability.']	(105464, 105561)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	544	['This statistic indexes the extent to which the phenotypic correlation between X and Y is mediated genetically.']	(105562, 105672)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	545	['That is, univariate heritability is the extent to which the variance of a trait can be explained by genetic variance; bivariate heritability is the extent to which the covariance between two traits (or the same trait at two ages) can be explained by genetic covariance.']	(105673, 105942)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	546	['Bivariate heritability is the genetic correlation (see the next paragraph) weighted by the product of the square roots of the heritabilities of X and Y and divided by the phenotypic correlation between the two traits (Plomin & DeFries, 1979).']	(105943, 106185)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	547	['The rest of the phenotypic correlation is explained by bivariate shared environment and bivariate nonshared environment.']	(106186, 106306)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	548	['In addition, the paths from the model can be transformed to obtain the estimates of genetic, shared, and non-shared environmental correlations between each pair of factors.']	(106307, 106479)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	549	['Genetic correlations index the extent to which genetic influences on one measure correlate with genetic influences on a second measure.']	(106480, 106615)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	550	['In other words, genetic correlations indicate the extent to which individual differences in the two measures reflect the same genetic influences.']	(106616, 106761)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	551	['This correlated factors model is illustrated inFigure 5—it is merely an algebraic transformation of the Cholesky model shown inFigure 4.']	(106762, 106898)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	552	['The point is that there are two important statistics: bivariate heritability which is the genetic contribution to the phenotypic correlation between traits, and the genetic correlation which is the extent to which genetic effects on one trait are correlated with genetic effects on another trait.']	(106899, 107195)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	553	['Multivariate genetic analyses are the topic of Chapter VI; Chapter V presents longitudinal genetic analyses based on similar models.']	(107196, 107328)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	554	['Caption (FIG): FIGURE 5\n\n—Correlated factors model.']	(107330, 107381)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	555	['Genetic correlations ( rA1A2) index the extent to which individual differences in the two measures reflect the same genetic influences.']	(107382, 107517)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	556	['Shared and nonshared environmental correlations index the extant to which the same environmental influences affect the two traits.']	(107518, 107648)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	557	['It is also possible to extend DF extremes analysis to address multivariate issues (Light & DeFries, 1995;Plomin & Kovas, 2005), analyzing two traits at the same measurement occasion, or the same trait at two measurement occasions.']	(107649, 107879)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	558	["In Chapter VI, probands were selected on the basis of being in the lowest 15% of web-based reading and mathematics scores at 10 years and analyzed in comparison to their co-twin's reading and mathematics scores."]	(107880, 108091)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	559	['Group heritability indicates the extent to which genetic factors account for the mean difference between probands selected on reading and the population on mathematics.']	(108092, 108260)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	560	['In other words, group heritability in a multivariate extremes analysis indicates the extent to which genetic effects mediate the phenotypic covariance between reading disability and mathematics ability.']	(108261, 108463)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	561	['The group genetic correlation indicates the extent to which the same genetic effects operate on reading disability and mathematics ability.']	(108464, 108603)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	562	["Analysis in both directions is required to estimate a DF extremes genetic correlation—that is, probands were also selected from the lowest 15% of mathematics performance and analyzed with their co-twin's quantitative trait scores on reading."]	(108604, 108845)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	563	['The group genetic correlation can be calculated using the following formula:whereis the group heritability from reading ( x ) to mathematics ( y ),is the group heritability from mathematics to reading,is the group heritability of reading, andis the group heritability of mathematics (seeKnopik, Alarcón, & DeFries, 1997for details).']	(108846, 109178)

PMC2784897.nxml.gz	667	['However, a less interesting, but more parsimonious, hypothesis is that the nonsignificant differences in shared environmental estimates for NC ratings by same versus different teachers are not real.']	(132030, 132228)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	668	['As was the case for same-teacher and different-teacher NC ratings, heritability estimates for the test-score data were similar for members of twin pairs, whether taught by the same teacher or by different teachers: .68 and .78 for TOWRE composite, .38 and .42 for PIAT, .52 and .46 for web-based Mathematics composite, and .35 and .41 for “ g ” on average across the 3 years.']	(132229, 132604)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	669	['Twin correlations and model-fitting estimates for same- and different-teacher ratings are available from the authors.']	(132605, 132722)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	670	['Because results are generally similar for twins taught by the same teacher and those taught by different teachers, subsequent analyses were conducted on the combined sample in order to maximize power and simplicity of presentation.']	(132723, 132954)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	671	['SUMMARY\nIndividual differences in early academic performance show substantial genetic influence and modest shared environmental influence.']	(132956, 133094)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	672	['The magnitude of genetic influence—about 65% for year-long teacher assessments based on U.K. NC criteria and about 55% for test data—is surprising.']	(133095, 133242)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	673	['Heritabilities are greater for academic performance than for general cognitive ability (35% on average).']	(133243, 133347)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	674	['We were also surprised to find such consistently high heritabilities of academic performance at 7, 9, and 10 years despite major changes in content across these years.']	(133348, 133515)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	675	['Our hypothesis was not confirmed that heritability would increase during the early school years as skills training developed into the application of these skills—for example, from learning to read to reading to learn.']	(133516, 133733)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	676	['Even though heritabilities are as high at 7 years as at 9 years, it is possible that the genetic correlates of academic performance reflect changing patterns of component skills from 7 to 9 years.']	(133734, 133930)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	677	['We will return to this issue in Chapter VI.']	(133931, 133974)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	678	['High heritabilities were not only found across all three ages but also across the components of each domain (e.g., for English: Speaking and Listening, Reading, and Writing) and across domains (English, Mathematics, and Science).']	(133975, 134204)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	679	['However, this finding does not imply that the same genetic factors affect these diverse domains of academic performance; multivariate genetic analysis is needed to address this issue (see Chapter VI).']	(134205, 134405)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	680	['An interesting sideline is that estimates of heritability were similar for teacher assessments when the same teacher assesses members of a twin pair and when different teachers assess them.']	(134406, 134595)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	681	['The similarity of results across domains, across ages, and across methods of assessment indicates the robustness of these findings.']	(134596, 134727)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	682	['Just as surprising is the modest role for shared environmental influence for pairs of children growing up in the same family and being taught in the same school, often by the same teacher in the same classroom.']	(134728, 134938)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	683	['Measures of general cognitive ability showed more shared environmental influence than do teacher assessments and tests of academic performance.']	(134939, 135082)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	684	['Nevertheless, nonshared environment accounts for more variance than shared environment, although it should be acknowledged again that nonshared environment also includes variance due to measurement error.']	(135083, 135287)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	685	['Results are similar for boys and girls, as well as for same-sex and opposite-sex DZ twins.']	(135288, 135378)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	686	['These results suggest that quantitative and qualitative sex differences do not play a major role in the origins of individual differences in learning abilities.']	(135379, 135539)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	687	['These results raise several questions to which we will return in the final chapter.']	(135540, 135623)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	688	['For example, why do teacher ratings of academic performance show greater genetic influence than test scores?']	(135624, 135732)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	689	['Why do tests of academic performance show more genetic influence and less shared environmental influence than measures of general cognitive ability?']	(135733, 135881)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	690	['Why is the TOWRE measure of word recognition at 7 years significantly more heritable than the PIAT reading recognition at 10 years?']	(135882, 136013)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	691	['Although these analyses of genetic and environmental influences on individual differences in learning abilities in the early school years have yielded some surprising results, the main goal of this monograph is to go beyond these rudimentary issues of nature and nurture in order to address the relationship between the normal and abnormal, longitudinal analyses of stability and change, and multivariate analyses of covariance within and between domains.']	(136014, 136469)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	692	['These are the topics of the next three chapters.']	(136470, 136518)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	693	['IV.']	(136520, 136523)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	694	['THE ABNORMAL IS NORMAL\nThe previous chapter investigated the sources of individual differences throughout the normal distribution, which we refer to as learning abilities.']	(136524, 136695)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	695	['In this chapter, we focus on the lower end of the distribution, learning disabilities.']	(136696, 136782)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	696	['To what extent are learning disabilities etiologically distinct from the normal range of variation?']	(136783, 136882)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	697	['Our hypothesis follows from Quantitative Trait Locus (QTL) theory, which posits that genetic influence on common disorders and complex traits is caused by many genes (loci) of small effect rather than by one gene or even by a few genes of large effect (Plomin, Owen, & McGuffin, 1994).']	(136883, 137168)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	698	['Unlike single-gene effects, that are necessary and sufficient for the development of a disorder, QTLs contribute interchangeably and additively as probablilistic risk factors.']	(137169, 137344)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	699	['If QTL theory is correct, common disorders such as learning disabilities are likely to be the quantitative extreme of the same genetic factors responsible for variation throughout the distribution.']	(137345, 137542)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	700	['The QTL model refers to quantitative traits even in relation to disorders because if many genes affect a disorder, then it necessarily follows that there will be a quantitative distribution rather than a dichotomy.']	(137543, 137757)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	701	['Stated more provocatively, there is no disability, just low ability—the abnormal is normal.']	(137758, 137849)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	702	['The QTL model is discussed in greater detail in Chapter VII.']	(137850, 137910)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	703	['The ultimate proof of the QTL model will come when QTLs identified for learning disabilities are found to be associated with the normal range of variation in abilities and vice versa.']	(137911, 138094)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	704	['With a large and representative twin sample like TEDS, it is possible to study disabilities in the context of abilities.']	(138095, 138215)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	705	['We can compare estimates of genetic and environmental influence for abilities and for disabilities in the same sample.']	(138216, 138334)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	706	['Finding that genetic or environmental estimates differ for abilities and disabilities indicates that there are etiological differences between abilities and disabilities.']	(138335, 138505)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	707	['However, differences in the magnitude of genetic and environmental estimates for abilities and disabilities are merely quantitative differences, not qualitative differences.']	(138506, 138679)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	708	['That is, even if heritability differed quantitatively for disabilities and abilities, the same genes could nonetheless be associated with disabilities and abilities.']	(138680, 138845)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	709	['Conversely, heritabilities could be the same for disabilities and abilities and yet different genes could be associated with disabilities and abilities.']	(138846, 138998)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	710	['What we would most like to know is whether there are qualitative differences between disabilities and abilities.']	(138999, 139111)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	711	['That is, are genes associated with learning disability different from the genes associated with normal variation in ability?']	(139112, 139236)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	712	['As discussed in Chapter II, we believe that DF extremes analysis addresses this issue of qualitative differences directly.']	(139237, 139359)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	713	['Finding group heritability implies genetic links between disabilities and abilities, and finding group shared environment implies shared environmental links between disabilities and abilities.']	(139360, 139552)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	714	['The structure of this chapter is similar to the previous chapter.']	(139553, 139618)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	715	['However, instead of presenting twin correlations and ACE model fitting for the entire sample, this chapter focuses on twin concordances, twin “group” correlations, and DF extremes analyses for children with low scores.']	(139619, 139837)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	716	['For reasons discussed in the previous chapter, we maximized power for these extremes analyses by combining boys and girls, same-sex, and opposite-sex DZ twins, and assessments by same and different teachers.']	(139838, 140045)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	717	['Furthermore, because results presented in the previous chapter were so similar for components of each domain, the present chapter simplifies presentation of results by focusing on the composite scores for each domain at each age.']	(140046, 140275)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	718	['We selected twin pairs for whom at least one twin scored in the lowest 15% on the composite score.']	(140276, 140374)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	719	['We have also conducted analyses using a 5% cut-off and generally find similar results.']	(140375, 140461)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	720	['We have chosen to present results for the 15% cut-off for three reasons.']	(140462, 140534)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	721	['First, performance one standard deviation below the mean, which corresponds to a 15.9% cut-off in a perfectly normal distribution, is often used as a cut-off for common disorders.']	(140535, 140714)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	722	['Second, for the U.K. National Curriculum (NC), a 15% cut-off corresponds to children identified as performing below their grade expectation and failing items that are solved correctly by the majority of much younger children (Kovas, Haworth, Petrill, & Plomin, in press).']	(140715, 140986)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	723	['Third, in TEDS, a 15% cut-off strikes a balance between extremity of scores and sample size needed to attain reasonable power in DF extremes analysis.']	(140987, 141137)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	724	['The number of probands in the lowest 15% of the distribution for each measure and the number of MZ and DZ pairs with at least one proband are listed in the footnote toTable 12.']	(141138, 141314)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	725	['In our individual differences analyses in the previous chapter, we excluded all twin pairs in which one or both twins scored 3 or more standard deviations below or above the mean so that our individual differences results would not be affected by very extreme scores.']	(141315, 141582)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	726	['However, children with low extreme scores were restored for the present analyses because our focus here is on children with low scores and because DF extremes analysis is an analysis of means rather than variances and analyses of means are not inordinately affected by outliers.']	(141583, 141861)

PMC2784897.nxml.gz	771	['DF extremes analysis is based on means for the probands, the co-twins, and the population in which probands were selected from the lowest 15% of the distribution, whereas analyses from the previous chapter are based on individual differences throughout the distribution.']	(148451, 148721)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	772	['Caption (FIG): FIGURE 6\n\n—Comparison between genetic and environmental estimates for the lowest-performing 15% (DF extremes analyses from this chapter) and for individual differences throughout the entire distribution (Chapter III).']	(148723, 148955)

PMC2784897.nxml.gz	774	['If ACE estimates had differed for disabilities and abilities, this would indicate an etiological difference between them.']	(149130, 149251)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	775	['However, as noted earlier and explained in Chapter II, such quantitative differences, for example in heritability, could be due to the same genes affecting disabilities and abilities but differing in the magnitude of their effect at the low end of the distribution.']	(149252, 149517)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	776	['This could occur, for example, if shared environmental influences had a stronger effect at the low end of the distribution.']	(149518, 149641)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	777	['In the present situation in which similar quantitative ACE estimates were found for disabilities and abilities, it is possible that different genes are associated with disabilities and abilities even though the net effects of such genes are of a similar magnitude for disabilities and abilities.']	(149642, 149937)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	778	['As mentioned earlier, what we would like to know is whether there are qualitative differences between disabilities and abilities; that is, whether different genes or different environmental factors affect disabilities and abilities.']	(149938, 150170)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	779	['We suggest that DF extremes analysis itself, not the comparison between the results for DF extremes analysis and analysis of individual differences, speaks to qualitative differences between disabilities and abilities.']	(150171, 150389)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	780	['Although this is a complicated issue (for details, seePlomin & Kovas, 2005), group heritability and group shared environment can be observed only to the extent that there are links between disability and ability.']	(150390, 150602)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	781	["That is, if the measure of disability is unrelated to the measure of ability, there can be no group heritability or group shared environment—the co-twins' mean would regress back to the population mean in DF extremes analysis."]	(150603, 150829)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	782	['However, finding genetic and environmental links between disability and ability does not imply that all effects are in common.']	(150830, 150956)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	783	['Indeed it is likely that there are rare single-gene effects and rare environmental trauma that lead to learning disability but account for little variance in the population as a whole (Plomin & Kovas, 2005).']	(150957, 151164)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	784	['Thus, we conclude that the results presented in this chapter are consistent with the hypothesis that the abnormal is normal both quantitatively and qualitatively.']	(151165, 151327)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	785	['The strongest test of the hypothesis that the abnormal is normal will come when genes are found that are associated with disabilities or abilities.']	(151328, 151475)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	786	['Our prediction is that any gene associated with reading disability, for example, will also be associated with individual differences in reading ability throughout the distribution, including good readers.']	(151476, 151680)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	787	['This hypothesis is consistent with QTL theory, which posits that common disorders are the quantitative extreme of the same genetic factors that create variation throughout the distribution.']	(151681, 151870)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	788	['It is possible that these conclusions do not apply to more severe forms of disability identified by different criteria and more severe cut-offs than the ones used in this study.']	(151871, 152048)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	789	['However, as discussed earlier, the cut-off for disability used in this study selected children with very low performance, failing items that are successfully solved by the majority of younger children.']	(152049, 152250)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	790	['Also, as discussed earlier, we repeated all analyses with a more severe cut-off of 5% of the whole sample, and obtained very similar results.']	(152251, 152392)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	791	['Finally, it is possible that some rare variants of learning disabilities have a distinct etiology from that of most common disabilities, as discussed in the concluding chapter.']	(152393, 152569)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	792	['Although the results reported in this chapter have no immediate implications for teaching or for remediating disabilities, we believe that it is important for parents, teachers and policy makers to recognize that common learning disabilities are etiologically the low end of quantitative continua of ability, rather than being driven by unique genetic and environmental factors.']	(152570, 152948)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	793	['This finding has far-reaching implications for defining learning disability as well as for research into factors that are responsible for learning disability, as discussed in Chapter VII.']	(152949, 153136)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	794	['SUMMARY\nACE results for the lowest 15% of children at each age for all measures are remarkably similar to the individual differences results presented in the previous chapter for the entire distribution.']	(153138, 153341)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	795	['That is, for disability as well as for ability, heritability for NC teacher ratings is very high (≈.70) and shared environment is very low (≈.10).']	(153342, 153488)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	796	['For web-based measures of reading and mathematics at 10 years as well as for tests of “ g ” at all three ages, disability as well as ability shows less heritability (≈.40) and more shared environmental influence (≈.20).']	(153489, 153708)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	797	['The similarity of ACE results for disability and ability indicates that the quantitative etiologies of disability and ability are similar.']	(153709, 153847)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	798	['The group heritability estimates suggest that the etiologies of disability and ability are also similar qualitatively.']	(153848, 153966)

PMC2784897.nxml.gz	919	['The genetic contribution to the correlation between NC English at 7 and 9 years is .46, the product of √.67 and √.32.']	(173764, 173881)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	920	['From the basic multivariate genetic model, this genetic contribution to the phenotypic correlation can be shown to be equivalent to the product of the square roots of the heritabilities of the two variables and their genetic correlation (Plomin & DeFries, 1979).']	(173882, 174144)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	921	['Knowing the heritabilities of the two variables, we can solve for the genetic correlation simply by dividing by the product of the square roots of the heritabilities.']	(174145, 174311)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	922	['For example, the heritabilities for NC English are .67 at 7 years and .65 at 9 years.']	(174312, 174397)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	923	['Dividing .46 (the genetic contribution to the phenotypic correlation) by the product of the square roots of their heritabilities estimates the genetic correlation as .70 [.46÷(√.67 ×√.65)=.70], which is exactly the same as the model-fitting estimate of the genetic correlation shown inTable 13.']	(174398, 174692)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	924	['The genetic correlations inTable 13are substantial.']	(174693, 174744)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	925	['Even between 7 and 10 years, the genetic correlations are .67 for NC English, .68 for NC mathematics, and .72 for “ g .” Even TOWRE at 7 years and PIAT at 10 years yield a high genetic correlation of .60 despite their significant differences in heritability and their modest phenotypic correlation.']	(174745, 175043)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	926	['In what is becoming a familiar pattern, NC science is the odd one out; in this case, it shows the lowest genetic correlation inTable 13, indicating greater qualitative differences in genetic influences from 9 to 10 years.']	(175044, 175265)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	927	['Unlike bivariate ACE estimates which sum to the phenotypic correlation, ACE correlations are independent; they could all be zero or they could all be unity.']	(175266, 175422)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	928	['InTable 13, the C correlations are in fact quite similar to the A correlations for NC English and mathematics: Between 7 and 10 years, the C correlations are .71 for NC English and .52 for NC mathematics.']	(175423, 175627)

PMC2784897.nxml.gz	930	['NC science yields the highest C correlation: 1.0 between 9 and 10 years.']	(175700, 175772)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	931	['TOWRE reading at 7 years and PIAT reading at 10 years yield a C correlation of .45.']	(175773, 175856)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	932	['The significant but modest E correlations for all measures (except “ g ”) suggest that nonshared environment is not completely due to error of measurement, which would not be expected to be stable longitudinally.']	(175857, 176069)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	933	['Longitudinal DF Extremes Analysis\nIn the previous chapter, DF extremes analyses indicated that the abnormal is normal.']	(176071, 176189)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	934	['As described in Chapter II and illustrated in Chapter IV, univariate DF extremes analysis begins with probands selected for extreme scores (or diagnoses) and analyzes how similar the mean of their co-twins is to the mean of the probands on a quantitative measure.']	(176190, 176453)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	935	['By comparing MZ and DZ co-twin means, “group” heritability can be estimated indicating the extent to which the mean quantitative trait score difference between the probands and the population can be attributed to genetic influence.']	(176454, 176685)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	936	['Univariate DF extremes analysis can be extended to bivariate analysis (Light & DeFries, 1995;Plomin & Kovas, 2005).']	(176686, 176801)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	937	['Although not discussed previously, the same considerations apply to the application of DF extremes analysis to longitudinal data—rather than analyzing two traits at the same measurement occasion, we can analyze the same trait at two measurement occasions.']	(176802, 177057)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	938	['Longitudinal DF extremes analysis can address the issue of genetic mediation of continuity and change for learning disabilities rather than for learning abilities—that is, for the low-performing extremes rather than for individual differences throughout the entire distribution.']	(177058, 177336)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	939	['This form of analysis is especially relevant for the goal of understanding both the antecedents and the long-term consequences of early disability, an issue with theoretical and applied significance.']	(177337, 177536)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	940	['A brief description of bivariate DF extremes analysis follows.']	(177537, 177599)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	941	['In contrast to univariate DF extremes analysis which selects probands as extreme on X and compares the quantitative scores of their MZ and DZ co-twins on X , bivariate DF extremes analysis selects probands on X and compares the quantitative scores of their co-twins on Y , a cross-trait twin group correlation.']	(177600, 177910)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	942	['The genetic contribution to the phenotypic difference between the means of the probands on trait X and the population on Y can be estimated by doubling the difference between the cross-trait twin group correlations for MZ and DZ twins.']	(177911, 178146)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	943	['Bivariate group heritability is the ratio between this genetic estimate and the phenotypic difference between the probands on trait X and the population on Y .']	(178147, 178306)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	944	['Unlike bivariate analysis of individual differences in unselected samples, such as those done earlier in this chapter, bivariate DF extremes analysis is directional in the sense that selecting probands on X and examining quantitative scores of co-twins on Y could yield different results as compared with selecting probands on Y and examining quantitative scores of co-twins on X .']	(178307, 178688)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	945	['A group genetic correlation can be derived from four group parameter estimates: bivariate group heritability estimated by selecting probands for X and assessing co-twins on Y , bivariate group heritability estimated by selecting probands for Y and assessing co-twins on X , and univariate group heritability estimates for X and for Y (seeKnopik, Alarcón, & DeFries, 1997).']	(178689, 179061)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	946	['The group genetic correlation is the most informative summative index of genetic effects on low extremes in a longitudinal context.']	(179062, 179193)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	947	['Although it is possible to conduct similar analyses of environmental influences, in this example of a longitudinal DF extremes analysis we will focus on genetic influences.']	(179194, 179366)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	948	['As an example of bivariate DF extremes analysis applied for the first time to longitudinal data, we analyzed the relationship between 7-year TOWRE scores and 10-year PIAT scores.']	(179367, 179545)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	949	["Because bivariate DF extremes analyses are bidirectional, we conducted two separate analyses: (1) selecting children in the lowest 15% of 7-year TOWRE and analyzing their co-twins' scores on the 10-year PIAT (TOWRE → PIAT), and (2) selecting children in the lowest 15% of 10-year PIAT and analyzing their co-twins' scores on the 7-year TOWRE (PIAT→TOWRE)."]	(179546, 179901)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	950	['For the TOWRE→PIAT longitudinal analysis, the phenotypic group correlation was .58, indicating that children with low scores on the TOWRE at 7 also had low scores on the PIAT at 10–they were 1.5 SD below the mean on the TOWRE at 7 and .87 SD below the mean on the PIAT at 10.']	(179902, 180177)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	951	['Bivariate group heritability was .61; that is, most of the phenotypic group correlation between the TOWRE and PIAT is mediated genetically.']	(180178, 180317)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	952	['In other words, genetic factors explain 61% of the difference between the mean TOWRE score of probands at 7 years and the population mean on the PIAT at 10 years.']	(180318, 180480)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	953	['That is, to the extent the low scores (lowest 15%) on the TOWRE predict below average scores on the PIAT 3 years later, this is due in large part to common genetic factors.']	(180481, 180653)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	954	['As noted earlier, the results of bi-directional bivariate extremes analyses need not be symmetrical, as is the case for the analysis of PIAT→TOWRE.']	(180654, 180801)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	955	['The phenotypic group correlation was .39 and bivariate group heritability was only .37, which indicates that genetic factors explain 37% of the difference between the mean PIAT score of probands at 10 years and the population mean on the TOWRE at 7 years.']	(180802, 181057)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	956	['Nonetheless, combining the results for the TOWRE→PIAT and the PIAT→TOWRE analyses yielded a genetic correlation of .90.']	(181058, 181177)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	957	['This suggests that despite the differences in bivariate group heritability for the two analyses, the genetic correlation is substantial between the extremes of TOWRE at 7 years and PIAT at 10 years.']	(181178, 181376)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	958	['Table 14compares these results from our longitudinal analyses at the extreme to those presented earlier in this chapter for the entire distribution.']	(181377, 181525)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	959	['Although it is noteworthy that there is lower bivariate heritability at the extremes than across the full distribution, the key result is the genetic correlation, which provides the strongest evidence for genetic links between low scores on the TOWRE at 7 years and low scores on the PIAT at 10 years.']	(181526, 181827)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	960	['Because the TOWRE and PIAT are so different (word recognition versus reading comprehension), this analysis could be viewed as a multivariate analysis as well as a longitudinal analysis.']	(181828, 182013)

PMC2784897.nxml.gz	1074	['MULTIVARIATE GENETIC MODEL-FITTING ANALYSIS\nAs described in the previous chapter in relation to longitudinal analysis and more generally in Chapter II, multivariate genetics can be used to analyze the covariance between traits rather than the variance of each trait separately in order to estimate the extent to which the same (common) or different (unique) genetic and environmental factors affect the traits.']	(192470, 192880)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	1075	['We will not repeat here the description of the two basic sets of statistics of multivariate genetic analysis: bivariate ACE statistics that specify the extent to which the phenotypic correlation is mediated by genetic and environmental factors, and ACE correlations that index the extent to which the same genetic and environmental factors affect the traits regardless of their effect on the phenotype.']	(192881, 193283)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	1076	['The only difference in approach is that the model used in most of the analyses in this chapter is a correlated factors model rather than a Cholesky model.']	(193284, 193438)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	1077	['The Cholesky model is most suited to variables that can be ordered, as was the case for the longitudinal analyses presented in the previous chapter in which the variables can be ordered by age.']	(193439, 193632)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	1078	['The correlated factors model is more appropriate for most of the multivariate genetic analyses in this chapter because it is not dependent on the order in which the variables are included and it also has the advantage of focusing on ACE correlations.']	(193633, 193883)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	1079	['We do use the Cholesky model in the final set of analyses in which we enter “ g ” first in order to investigate the multivariate ACE structure of academic performance in reference to “ g .” Despite these presentational issues, it should be kept in mind that the correlated factors model and the Cholesky model are algebraic transformations of each other and thus the same bivariate ACE estimates and ACE correlations can be obtained from the two models, as illustrated in the previous chapter.']	(193884, 194377)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	1080	['InFigure 9, the three components of NC English at 7 years are used as an example of a correlated factor model-fitting analysis, with results depicted as a path diagram.']	(194378, 194546)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	1081	['In the first panel, the coefficients from the latent A variables to the measured traits (shown in rectangles) are heritability estimates (the path coefficient itself is the square root of this heritability).']	(194547, 194754)
PMC2784897.nxml.gz	1082	['The analogous coefficients in the other panels are shared (C) and nonshared (E) environment contributions to variance.']	(194755, 194873)

PMC2784897.nxml.gz	1270	['Our multivariate genetic analyses between learning abilities and general cognitive ability (“ g ”) suggest that some generalist genes that affect learning abilities are even more general in that they also affect other sorts of cognitive abilities included in “ g .” However, generalist genes are not just “ g ” because learning abilities are more strongly correlated genetically with each other than they are with “ g .” About a third of the genetic variance of English and mathematics is in common with “ g ,” about a third of the genetic variance is general to academic performance independent of “ g ,” and about a third is specific to each domain.']	(225480, 226131)
